The Spiritual Jesus proclaimed almost entirely by Paul, who claimed to have seem the Spirit of Jesus in a blinding flash of light, and was henceforth changed from an enemy of Jesus followers to the most dedicated of believers. This Jesus was described in Paul's letters, which were the first written of the books compiled for the \\"New Testament\\". This Jesus became the one most familiar to Gentiles, and was described years after Paul's death in the Gospel of John. B001NLKWLW|4.0|Every so often a book comes along that inspires me both as a reader and a writer. Kate Morton's, The Forgotten Garden, is one of those books.<br /><br />The Forgotten Garden spans over a hundred years and two different countries. It traces three generations and is told from at least eight different viewpoints, and yet the story flows seamlessly.  As an exercise in plotting, this book reminds me of Audrey Niffenegger's, Time Traveller's Wife; another book that manages to effortlessly jump back and forwards in time without leaving the reader behind. Given the complexity though, this is a book that rewards a reader with time to devote to extended periods of reading. It really doesn't lend itself to reading in small bites.<br /><br />Over the course of the novel, Morton unravels a tangled collection of motivations and events that conspire to see a four year old girl abandoned on a ship bound for Australia. As subsequent generations search for meaning and identity, so too does the reader follow a trail of breadcrumbs leading to the ultimate truth. One of the great pleasures of this book is seeing the way seemingly inconsequential details have a powerful impact on events. While occasionally there are elements that you know are going to be relevant in chapters to come, others come as a surprise. By two thirds of the way through the book I assumed I knew how it would turn out, but there were enough unresolved threads to the story, that it could possibly have veered off in a different direction.<br /><br />If there is any weakness to the story, it's that the male characters are nowhere near as well drawn as the female. For the most part they are bit players in the unfolding of the dramas of the central women in the story and their stories don't seem to unfold as cleanly or feel quite as believable. Given their supporting role, however, this does not really impact on the reading experience.<br /><br />This is a very rewarding read and highly recommended.

B004IPPINW|3.0|I read this book a couple of weeks ago, but have delayed reviewing it, because I have been struggling to decide whether I liked it or not.<br /><br />I'm still not entirely sure, so let's just start at the beginning.<br /><br />Written in the first person, The Reluctant Fundamentalist, is told from the perspective of Changez, a young Pakistani man who narrates the story of his life to an unnamed American over dinner. His life's story is one of journeys; from Lahore to Princeton, to a New York firm, through a failed relationship, the fallout of 9/11, and a return to Pakistan. There are underlying streams of pain and unrest. Changez is an outsider who struggles to find his away into American life until he is ultimately rejected and rejects all that an American life means. And finally, the man who was once a Princeton shining star becomes a radical.<br /><br />The novel speaks to the pain and rejection of being within American society and then suddenly becoming an outsider. It is a novel of aspiration and disappointment and yet, I never really felt that it captured the essence of that experience. I could understand the narrator's pain and rejection at losing his girlfriend, but I never really had a clear sense of why Changez was so quick to turn on his adopted country.<br /><br />In many ways the premise of this novel reminds me of the books that are being written about the internment of Japanese during World War II. In both cases people have gone from being entwined in a local community to overnight pariahs. Perhaps it is with the benefit of seventy years of hindsight that what is written about the Japanese is able to explore the communities' pain on a more subtle and open level. For its part, The Reluctant Fundamentalist, feels like it has been written with caution. The pain is there but there seems a reluctance to really dig down into it.<br /><br />Perhaps the simplest way to describe my feelings about the novel is that I really admire what it has set out to do - to tell another side of the 9/11 experience. I like too that it doesn't offer easy answers; but still I wanted more. My hope is that this book opens the door to other novels exploring this event and experience from a different perspective. There are many stories to be told, and with each telling comes a different way of understanding. The more diverse the narrative being told, perhaps the greater the chance of us changing the way our societies interact with each other and with their own, in the future.o tell another side of the 9/11 experience. The 180g vinyls are nice and heavy and have played with minimal popping and crackling on the first few plays. The vinyls are packaged in plastic sleeves which tend to leave small bits of plastic on the record: this is not much of a problem, but it does need to be brushed off before playing almost every time.<br /><br />If all vinyl releases (and re-releases) nowadays were of this quality, I would almost certainly leave CD's behind. 140006922X|3.0|This is an easy and light read, enjoyable for anyone interested in the historical Jesus. The author has compiled modern biblical research into an easily digestible book. There is a certain repetitiveness with regards to the listing of the other Messiahs that came before and after Jesus of Nazareth. This rhetorical technique to relegate Jesus as one of many fails to be interesting by the second and third re-telling. The book's major flaw however, is the failure to really convince on the titular premise of Jesus as zealot. This notion is almost a secondary aspect to the historical study and is tacked ad-hoc in various places in the book when the author seems to remember the purported central thesis. Far from establishing Jesus as a zealous revolutionary, the author seems confounded with even producing a coherent guess as to the meaning of the Jesus sobriquet - Son of Man, which he claims is the key to understanding Jesus's description of himself. Note the subheading, the life and times of Jesus of Nazareth, much of the book is devoted to the 'times'  of Jesus, these for me made up the most interesting parts of the work.

In accepting his fate, Meursault acknowledges that he has no control over his life. As he says, it will all inevitably end in death, now or twenty years from now. Those men and women who deliberate over and ultimately decide the course of Meursault's life have only the illusion of control as they spew empty notions of responsibility, remorse, and love, an illusion that allows them to bear this life, which they are incapable of comprehending. Meursault is at least aware of his ignorance, finally surrendering to the conclusion that there is no point to life--or if there is, it is so far beyond our grasp that it is also beyond faith and belief in God, or any sorts of discussions about it. His fate to die earlier rather than later perhaps saves him from more years of fumbling about, pretending that when or whether he lives or dies has any purpose or meaning at all.

1452000247|5.0|Peter: A Darkened Fairytale by Author William O'Brien<br /><br />Peter, a delightful nine year old boy living in Bodenlea Park in a small village, is soon to turn ten. Just before his double digit birthday, he and his mother make a little visit to Mrs. Kipple's antique store. After having a cup of tea, Peter decides to browse through the store to help pass away the time while his mother and Mrs. Kipple enjoy their visit. He manages to find a well-worn and tattered wood box that piques his curiosity. It is being sold for a very minimal price and Peter eagerly asks his mother to buy it for him. With the price being so reasonable, Peter's mother readily purchases this quaint little box. As soon as they return home, Peter goes to his room and fiddles with the box and discovers a hidden drawer. Inside he finds three odd objects, a silver ball, a pineapple chunk, and a copper key...<br /><br />On his birthday he is invited back to Mrs. Kipple's shop because apparently, she has a special gift for him. He finds her in a small room by the staircase which he has never noticed before. In this room there is another smaller room about the size of a broom closet. The key opens the door and Peter is transported into a fantasy world which consists of a wide cast of magical characters. This fairytale world takes him on a unique excursion into the land of talking stone paths where toads and little boys turn into steam in order to get to other unique and captivating places. Peter is exposed to a very unusual and challenging journey.<br /><br />Talented Author William O'Brien has done a great job of tapping into the creative abilities of the mind in this well-written fictional novel. The descriptions are vivid and easy to imagine. The curiosity of Peter and the pleasure he experiences in this enthralling and oftentimes dark fairytale are sure to bring enjoyment to its reading audience. Mystical encounters in a magical kingdom sums up Peter's enchanting plight as he moves along and has many unexpected encounters. The thread of good versus evil that is common to most, if not all fairytales, is expertly woven into this captivating story which will be pleasing to a wide range of readers and a variety of ages...in essence to both children and adults.  The poems at the end of the book are an added bonus and give this novel a special touch and an even greater glimpse at the talent of Author William O'Brien. B003G4W49C|3.0|I enjoyed some aspects of the book. The story was easy to get into and kept me turning the pages. Some of the technological foresight was cool, and the psychological implications for Ender, the main character, were kind of interesting at times. I also found some of the themes thought provoking -- for example, using children as weapons of mass destruction, manipulation, isolation, fear-mongering, etc. and the video game sequences were a welcome breeze of creativity in the midst of prose that often was frankly dull.<br /><br />I just wish a better author had written the story. The constant shifting between narration, dialogue, and internal monologue -- by multiple characters nonetheless -- was often annoying. Card attempts to defend himself from 1st edition critics in his preface that he wanted the book to be accessible to a wide audience and makes a swipe at higher-brow writing. But I think he misses the point. Writing doesn't have to be weak in order for young adults to understand it.<br /><br />Worse than the prose issues though were the problems of characterization. Many of the characters in the Ender's Game had an almost identical voice and personality, making my impression of them simply muddle together. And the characters who do stand out typically do so in an overly simple and extreme way: Peter's malevolence, Bonzo's arrogance, Chamrajnagar's zaniness. I will say though that Valentine, Alai, and Bean weren't too badly constructed.<br /><br />The other main issue I have is that the plot is quite unbalanced and implausible. The battles and strategy at Battle School, while necessary, went through way too many iterations and became quite boring after a while. Yet the end of the book was considerably rushed even though it contained many of the more interesting ideas. The subplot of Peter and Valentine's take-over-the-world-by-pseudonymous-blogging was quite implausible (though I'd admit probably an exciting concept for a 12 year old reader) and was poorly developed at that.<br /><br />In my opinion, Ender's Game is a decent sci-fi novel for an adolescent. There is too much good science fiction out there to recommend it to anyone else though.

B00EYN3HTA|5.0|This enchanting poetry collection belongs to the genre of Fantasy and Fairyland. As a reader I was fascinated by the magical reality which the poet has created throughout the entire book. It felt like being inside a fairy tale, or inside a mythological journey, a fact which conveyed the reader with an epic and divine tang of eternity. Many of the poems were intuitive and somewhat mysterious, yet not without piety. The way the reader was swinging in moods was fantastic, as some poems were heavy whereas others were light and blithe. It means that the poet has the ability to dictate the mood apart from the wonderful rich description abound. I really enjoyed the light atmosphere in some of the euphoric poems. There was something divine and alleviating in them, perhaps even a tinge of novelty and romanticism alluding to the overall message of universal love. Some of the events were related to the story of Peter, whom is a chivalrous character, a savior that defeated the forces of darkness. The structure of the poems was interesting, as here the poet often used the frames of rhymes, but not above the message and not on the account of the wonderful enchanting drift. The language was rich and powerful, and together with the plays of words it imparted the poems with a spray of stardust. It is as if the poet has developed a new dialect in poetry, which is an indication of originality, intelligence, and evolution. His individual print was fantastic, as I often felt like a fairy was singing to me, or guiding me as a reader. There was a certain rhythm, which was compatible with the occurrences within the poems. The messages, most of which were implied, are associated with the poet's chief doctrine, that love, hope, and faith are the creators of magic, and that despite the fact that evil is surrounding us, we can always win over evil, providing that we do not lose hope, faith, or love. For me it has been a wonderful experience to read poetry in such a genre. It was truly unconventional, as many poets are serious and melancholy. This poet was not only happy; he was also a guide that leads to happiness. My mood was uplifted after reading this book. For me this book has been both a pleasure and a remedy.<br /><br />Poems analysis:<br /><br />In poem 1 'Drawers and Doors' I got the sense of traveling in space. Moreover, the reality of the subsequent poems was introduced through the images of mystery, as if there was a secret waiting to be discovered by the reader. The notion of time, together with the suspense of the unseen danger, just like in the fairy tale about Peter, played a chief role here in both the creation of a mysterious mood on one hand, and in the conveyance of the chief message of being alert, and not drift into the illusion that danger does not exist, on the other hand. In poem 2 `Finding Fingle' I enjoyed the rhymes and the panoramic description of a fairyland reality. The elf and the moonlight together with the motion of nature made me feel as if I was taking part in a fictional evolution of magic. The punch line in the last stanza also alludes as for the magic which is within the poet; i.e. to transform our world into a magical one, hence, the poet is depicted as a healer, owing to his good intentions. He is an ethicist and an exponent of nature and life. The rich descriptions of nature accelerates in Poem 4 `Shylong Woods', as here the first stanza contains a strong tangible object of nature in every line. Sky, stone, grass, frog, fish, and water, all capture the attention of the reader and ushers one into the reality of nature. Hence the magic is in nature itself, a fact which renders the poet a naturalist and a lover of the earth; all strong indications that he is also a Carpe Diem poet whose goal in life is to help mankind to save the earth. In the second stanza the seeds and the reeds proceed with the evolutionary description of magic. The third line breaks the momentum briefly to proceed in the subsequent line with a return to the beauty of nature through the Iris. The viewer is a traveling spectator that cherishes the beauties of nature. Moreover, the assertion in the first line of the third stanza of `always aware' alludes as for the overall message that one should always cherish nature, but at the same time be aware of unseen danger, and/or of unexpected events. Hence, the message here is not to be nave, and not to ignore the beauties of life at the same time. The poet advocates for life as a miracle, and as for the miracle of life in many of his poems. The last stanza was very interesting, as here the poet uses discernment in order to warn the reader as for the unseen danger. Poem 5 `Frozen Magic' gives the reader not even one moment of abstraction. The wonderful description of a fairy tale made it impossible for me to wander away. The reality which is imposed on the reader is simply wonderful; it is magical and rich, and here Peter's world is introduced with a warning. Stanza 4 in this poem was probably the most enchanting stanza in the entire collection. Poem 6  'Kiylore', however, is linked to poem 4, and to the overall message of waking up into the reality of the poet. The imaginary realism is the real world, the inner world is the way to salvation, not the dangers, not the facile world, hence, I was really moved by the method which the poet used here, and also in many of his other poems. Through sounds and through plays of words the call is to wake up into the fairyland's reality. The sounds' expressionism here was superb. The reality that the reader should seek is the dream world, the unseen beauties of life, not the worries of the rabble. For me the enhancement of the hearing sense brought me to the realization of the paradox of life. Here, the reader is asleep (in ignorance perhaps) in the beautiful world, hence it is all about magic and awareness, just like in the first poem, that the way to salvation is through a hidden path, a narrow gate, an unseen reality. I felt that the poet woke me up to a higher level of consciousness in many poems, thus, the way the poems were constructed in order to deliver a message was brilliant in the sense that the reader is not aware of the fact that the poet, is in fact, a shepherd and a pathfinder. The third line of the first stanza gave the poem a mood of a fairytale, whereas the word hidden in the third line of the second stanza emphasizes the fog of mystery and the atmosphere of magic. The secret, the magic, the mystery, all prods the reader to seek for the hidden treasure of carpe diem. Hence the poet advocates for the treasure of life in the spirit, a fact which renders him a divine messenger and an emissary of the light. The strong punch line in the second stanza, which is a chief message, reminds the reader that evil is always around us and even within us, but that it cannot vanquish us unless we yield to it. Hence, once more, the poet instructs us to remain aware of evil, not to deny it, but simultaneously to fight evil and defeat it with our will to live and find happiness in life. For me it was an injection of hope that brought me back to the will to find happiness in life. The first stanza of the subsequent poem (see poem 7) `A Truth' only solidifies the mood of alleviation as here the abstract elation with a tinge of romanticism and love uplifted me to the notion of nirvana. The `dreams come true' path, once more, is hidden, but it exists, and if we delve deep inside our souls we can find it, and by thus we can make it to the light. Peter in that context is the ordinary reader that seeks salvation. The assertions in this poem render the poet celestial, divine, and overwhelming. In poem 8 `Chantowald' I was impressed with the intensity and with the motion within the poem. It is as if the pace was rapidly exciting, the words crumble and rumbles in the third stanza gave it an aphoristic tinge, as so did the words by and play in the subsequent stanza. There was a wonderful rush of evolution, motion, emotion, and commotion that made me curious as for the end of the event. The structure was terse; however, the rhymes were interesting as they broke the symmetry of the 4 lines and 4 stanzas with their inconsistency, a fact which contributed to the diversity of the poem. In poem 9 `Cauldron' I enjoyed the rhymes and rhythm. The cadence was magnificent, hence together with the message, which appears in the last two lines of the last stanza, they rendered this poem very powerful. The poet's additional characteristics and virtues are exposed, as he is clearly sentimental, emotional, and above all a moralist. In the subsequent poem `The Fever' (see poem 10) I was impressed once more by the breaking of the rhymes in the first and the last stanzas. I believe this was done in order to deliver an important message that digresses from style on grounds of its hidden essence. In the first stanza the words cries and inside subtly distracted the reader form the subsequent rhymes, whereas in the end of the poem the words flash and pass, reminded the reader that it is not a poem of vanity (paradoxically order) and that the rhymes were not totally symmetric for a reason. My interpretation in this poem would be that it was done in order to keep the reader aware as for the reality of the poem. It means that if there were rhymes all the way to the end, in a symmetric perfection, then the reader would have fallen asleep, escape heed, and the poem would have lost its value and message. I find this poem constructed as a genius. The poet rebels against symmetry of rhymes, and the strong message is implied in the last stanza. Time is in constant motion. The miracle of life occurs due to motion, the earth was created owing to motion, and we live life owing to motion. William O'Brien is probably one of the most dynamic poets I have ever read. The message here, hence, is that time cannot stand still as long as we desire to live. Yes, the first line of the last stanza indicates the death has a hold on us, because of our physical existence, but our spirit lives forever, hence death is motionlessness, whereas life is motion. When the poet declares in the punch line that `clock will tick forever pass' he advocates for life, and for the triumph of life over death owing to motion. It should be noted that in his entire collection, motion was appreciated and exercised through the events and the descriptions of nature and fantasy. Hence this poet is an exponent of carpe diem and dynamics. Poem 11 `Cuthbert' reminded me somehow about the classic story of `Alice in Wonderland' (so did poem 39). Moreover, I just loved the rich English language, and I have no idea why I have thought about old Britain and its rich diversity in this poem. The tempo was great, particularly in the first stanza. Furthermore, the end in the last stanza amused me. Poem 13 `Brownies' was simply the epitome of a fairy tale poem. Apart from the motion in the poem, and apart from the strong first word in the poem, `Hidden', I was also compelled to by the intensity of the descriptions of the living creatures, both natural and mythological. Poem 14 `Devil's Wish' was a bit eerie, and here the mood was kind of dark and frightening. The poet can master the moods and create also an eerie and dark atmosphere, which is a faculty that requires a talent. It renders the poet versatile and compelling. There was like a chant, or a spell, hence a ceremony that reminded me of witches and warlocks etc. This poem was brilliantly constructed. It was probably the darkest poem in this book, no doubt, and apart from poem 42 I do not recall any other poems that frightened me at all. The poet is aware of the forces of darkness; he somehow knows them, a fact which terrified me a bit in regard to his image. But subsequent poems reassured me that he has the entire gamut of human emotions, and that his point is exactly that we must know evil, or rather be aware of it, before we can defeat it. The poet could have been a sorcerer, or a dark shepherd if he wanted to, but he chose the bright path of salvation. He chose altruism over selfishness. His empirical knowledge in regard to darkness, hence, can only benefit those who would follow him out of darkness and into the light. Poem 15 `Rickety Lodge' was extremely powerful. Once more the poet appears as an audible expressionist, by using the hearing sense he also deliver a strong message in the chorus. `Are you there?' means, once more, that one should focus on the real world, which is the fairyland reality. The poet hence is a master-mind that compels the reader to follow his instructions and advice, but all in a good spirit and for the higher purpose of salvation (light, life, love). The poem begins with a noise which only intensifies until the chorus. Furthermore, all three stanzas describe noises and tumult which only render the reader more curious about the source of it. The ghost, the host, the clock, the wind, and all the other movements in the poem gave the poem a tinge of madness. It was a brilliant poem in regard to event and motion. Poems 16-20 amazed me as they all appeared as savage, or somewhat predatory poems, aptly constructed in rhymes. I call them the heavy poems, because they had this heavy feeling of dread and even terror in them. In poem 16 `Zombie Queen' the first stanza was somewhat grizzly and savage. However, if I ever had a doubt as for the ongoing events, the third stanza reassured me that physical death and bodily harm are a fairly good reason to stay away from the Zombie Queen. The atmosphere of fear, and the `hunter and hunted' mood also appears in the subsequent poem `Bisquenta' However, I could not help stretching a smile of amusement, for what a pleasure that it was to hear the predator in poem 20 `Quengorn' describes what it will do to its victim in the last stanza. A predator with a sense of humor makes a lot of a difference. In fact, such a witty creation of character has a dual benefit, because it affects the mood of the reader positively on one hand, and transforms the predator from a flat character to a round character on the other hand. In 'Elves and Goblins' (Poem 21) I felt like I was reading a fairy tale in rhymes. Here, in the last stanza, there were some allusions as for the call of the poet. The words divine and prophecies reminded me that poetry is the true religion and that the ethicist poet is the divine messenger, hence the highest priest. Unlike the false choice of mankind, which is to choose ministers of religion and politics by power and social hierarchy, the poet is a free spirit, a divine messenger that proves his ministry through words, deeds, and merits. It is by merits that the poet is deemed a high priest, not by bias, and that is the beauty of poetry. Emerald rain was a term that strongly suggested that the viewer, hence the poet, is one with the creator in the establishment of colors and diversity. What a strong description in two words, for it was not merely a visual description, but rather emotional and uplifting one. Emerald, not only in color, but also in regard to the stone itself, was the notion which brought me higher to the Fairyland sky and reminded me about my own dreams on earth. The poet is so rich and powerful in creation that I have felt humbled as a reader throughout the entire book. In the subsequent poem, (poem 22), 'The Vaandorg Dragon' the warning against the existence of the dark forces resurfaces. In poem 23 `Wildflower Valley' I enjoyed the rhymes and the tempo. This poem reminded me of the old poets and their old English style. There was something romantic, natural, and light about it. The expression of `Oh' and the word `protection' rendered the poem positive. Here the mechanism of the earth is healing, and protected by magic. In `Devic Magic' (poem 24) I was enchanted by the rhymes and by the beautiful descriptions of a fairy world. The beauty of life is hidden and magical, but the poet keeps pointing it out until the reader is compelled to take a good look and learn that the true essence of aestheticism is inside us, inside nature. It is a clear message to cherish life and give respect to the earth. The poet is intoxicated with the love of life and nature, hence, his call to behold the beauties of the forest and the fairies is a call to follow Carpe Diem and love. The blithe feeling in this poem uplifted my mood tremendously. The final verse, however, reassure the believers that the reward is natural, hence that the law of karma, or universal law, has bee retained, and should be maintained by all exponents of the magic of the earth. It is by faith that we create miracles, invite miracles, and enjoy miracles. Hence, faith is the chief message in this lovely poem. As for poem 25 'The Gift', I must admit here that it is my favorite poem in this collection. The opening line of the first stanza has already enchanted me and swept me into this epic divine mood in which I could not escape the embrace of an everlasting bliss. Thereupon the tempo, sublimated, refined and consuming simply lead me to catharsis. It is without any doubt a chastity poem, rare, beautiful, and powerful. The great message in the last two lines spoke directly to me. Here I was guided to think positively, hence no matter how bad things are, we should always keep the flame of faith burning. Because owing to faith we can attain the goal of life. We can find happiness, hence I realized that the poet was a shepherd and an emissary of the light, a savior which leads to green pastures. The final line also implies as for altruism, which is one of the poet's myriad virtues. In the subsequent poem `Eternal' (poem 26) I was happy to discover a new style that had borne a great message. In my view the message was hidden in the first stanza, as the test mentioned is a test of faith. Once more the messages of `don't lose hope' and, `keep the flame of faith burning' resurfaced. The last line in the last stanza, moreover, advocates for the message of motion, hence that life in the spirit never dies. Eternity is implied also through the circle of life through growth and blossoms in nature itself. In `Floating by' (poem 27) I have enjoyed the tempo, the rhymes, and the flow. Moreover, the play of words in the last two lines of the third stanza was magnificent. As for the rhymes, it was an interesting development. The first stanza consisted of perfect rhymes, whereas the last line of the second stanza broke the momentum, as pure and more allowed the third stanza the freedom of being liberated from rhymes. However, there was a twist in the last stanza as here rhymes have returned but not in perfection. Thus we get contrasting stanzas, as stanza 1 and 3 are two complete opposites/contrasts in style, whereas stanzas 2 and 4 are compatible, because both have one imperfect rhyme. In stanza 4 it was, however, the second line that broke the perfection, as grand does not fully rhyme with lands. But the interesting fact was that the rebellious line in stanza 2 was line 4 whereas in stanza 4 the rebellious line was line 2. Thus, there is an order in chaos in regard to structure, patterns and style here, a fact which impressed me a great deal. Moreover, I enjoyed the play of words. The last stanza forged the poem epic. In `Twizzlebumps' (poem 28) I found joy in the chorus. Here the alliteration rendered the poem with a very light and jolly ambiance. In poem 29 `Chanted Spell' everything felt like a description excerpted from a fairy tale. The last stanza, and in particular the last line, imply as for the arrival of a new era, or of a new dawn. The dove which is a symbol equated with Peter and with the poet William, hence the poet is a new age hero of peace; i.e. a deliverer of a new vision. The vision of peace and love on earth, through the discovery of the inner magical world of nature and of mankind (fantasy). Hence, the divine intervention, is in fact, a poetic intervention. Hence, the poetry and literature of William O'Brien are the tools which are used by the individual to create a divine intervention, not from a thin air, but from the tangible physical world, which we ought to respect. The divine messenger is neither a politician nor a priest. The divine messenger is a poet, because neither religion nor politics are qualified to impart mankind with the knowledge  of salvation. It is literature in general, and poetry in particular, that is qualified to redeem mankind and save it from self destruction. The savior is a dove of peace, hence, an artist, an author, a poet. In poem 31 `Bubbles' I simply admire the end. The hidden message here was, in my view, breaking up illusions. The light movement, and particularly the drift in the second stanza swept me in the wind of hope. But once more, the poet warns us not be caught up in illusions, but to be aware of the reality around us. The end could also mean liberation from a trapped situation. In Poem 32, 'Jizzlebugs', the last line in the first stanza caught my attention. Here the words Googly and giggles reminded me of the poet's unique diction, rich jargon, and alliterative idiosyncrasy. It was a pleasure to read such poems throughout the entire book, even though I often took it for granted because of curiosity in regard to the messages. The reality was fantastic, and the last two lines of the poem filled my heart with hope and elation. The law of karma, destiny, and the laws of the universe surpassed my doubts and reminded me that everything in life happens for a reason. Hence, the poet is an advocate of equilibrium, equality, and salvation, and additionally an exponent of karma/divine law. The subsequent poem, poem 33, `The Prancing Witch' reminded me of psalms from the bible, yet the prayer, the hope, the request and supplications are not religious, but spiritual. The call is pure, chaste, kind, and untainted by the external reality of mankind. Hence, the poet is pure in both mission and purpose. It is a chastity poem, no doubt; pious, moral, and positive. I was also intrigued by this poem because of the messages of love, peace, and social harmony. The rhymed stanzas added to the beauty of the poem and to the sense of purity and healing. In `Coloured Arcs' (poem 34) I was enthused by the opening line and its strong play of words. Thereupon, I noticed that the opening line of each stanza consisted of a similar cadence and play of words. Alliteration appeared in many of the poems in this book, but here alliteration has intensified to a huge extent of beauty. I also enjoyed the theme of the colours and the rainbow, and their association with the alliterative style and flow of the poem. The final message has amused me, as here the poet alludes as for the message that the real gold is not the superstition of finding gold at the end of the rainbow. The real gold is inner; it is the spiritual gift and the love of life, etc. The message of carpe diem is the real gold, and therefore rich people often realize that they cannot find happiness despite of their money. Money is not the source of true happiness, love is. For me the end was a clear indication that the poet is a liberator of a biblical proportion. One can keep be deluded by the vanities of mankind, money, gold, power, but the poet deride the illusionists by telling them implicitly: you will not find happiness in gold, or money, or in any vanity, because the real treasure is not there. The real treasure is within us, within life, in human adventure, in human experience, in the journey of life, and in the right path, through the narrow gate, through the road, which he knows and lead. Follow the poet not the mirages of the rainbow, and you will find true happiness in life: that is the chief message here. In the subsequent two poems, `The light I+II (see poems 35 and 36) I enjoyed the description of the fairy and the magnificent magical panorama which is expressed by the poet. Here, alliteration, once again, captivated me. In poem 36, in the first stanza, line three, I felt that I was simply blown off by the fluttering foliage and felt that my spirit was soaring higher towards the sky. In poem 37, `Kipple' which is the first poem of the fourth part of the book, I was struck by the last stanza. Here I knew that the poet's advocacy for faith and confidence is the catalyst of miracles. The subsequent poems in the fourth part described characters and events from the fairy tale about Peter. However, poem 42 `The Man in the Field' gave me the spooks and reminded me how creepy and dark the world can be. Despite the fact that he is described as a lovely man in the third stanza, still, I have found him ghastly and uncanny. However, the last stanza was strong, the word God, and the end of the misery gave the poem a sense of a good end, catharsis, and/or a liberation from human sufferings. In the subsequent poem, `The fortress' (poem 43) I was comforted by the last stanza. As here, Peter, the hero, is going to overcome the ordeal, and by thus, provide all the readers with faith and hope that it does not matter how weak one is in society, one can always make it to happiness when one is willing to seize the day. It is another allusion as for the poets adherence to the Carpe Diem principle that life is precious. In `Little things' (poem 44) I have discovered that the message of the poet is that it is small details, hence small things in life, that lead us to great miracles. Like the parable of the mustard seed in the new testament, I have found out that the poetry and literature of William O'Brien were in fact the seeds of salvation. Just like the mustard seed they are about to explode with grace and beauty. Beneath the obvious, hence, beneath the rich descriptions of fiction and nature, and beneath the storyline there is a hidden divine treasure, waiting to grow up exponentially and save mankind from destruction. William's great vision to mankind is yet to be demystified. It is the vision of love and peace. In poem 51, `A Place of Knowing', which is the last poem of this fascinating collection, I have found the last two lines as the perfect message for sealing this book. Once more it is a Carpe Diem message, but here the message, unlike in many other poems, is neither hidden nor implied. The poet seals the book with an explicit declaration of motion, and here it is clear as crystal that miracles can only happen owing to motion. Remember, the earth without its constant revolution will die, the universe is also in constant motion, hence the call is to adapt to nature, to the earth, to the universe, and most of all to divine law which states that a man reaps in joy what he has sown with tears. William O'Brien has transported me to the heavenly realm in this book. Hence, I acted upon the final message and did not hesitate to write this review in a hope that mankind will recognize poets in general, and divine poets, like William O'Brien, in particular, as the true emissaries of the light, and as the loyal leaders of moral virtue. Salvation can only come from art, not from authority. Social harmony can only come from beauty not from power. World peace can only be attained through love and reason, not through fear and madness. This book was written with sparks of a divine inspiration. Why don't we just call it magic?<br /><br />Poet's additional characteristics: karmic, divine, liberating, fascinating, fantastic, chaste, pious, altruist, alliterative, profound, mysterious, peaceful, loving, hopefully, loyal, intriguing, enchanting, divine, ethical, discerning, insightful, expressive, kind.fit, because it affects the mood of the reader positively on one hand, and transforms the predator from a flat character to a round character on the other hand. B00DR98EYK|5.0|I'm always dubious when there is a lot of fuss and hype about a new album from an \\"exciting new band\\" because it's often just very ordinary stuff being cleverly marketed.  Not here, though.  I tried this without much expectation and was very impressed.<br /><br />London Grammar's sound is haunting and mournful with generally restrained but very smart production so that these songs shine like melancholy gems.  They are tuneful and quite varied, and although they're about well-worn themes they treat them with a lyrical originality which is very refreshing.  I find lines like \\"Stay awake with me...\\" very evocative, for example.  The instrumental work is very good, and Hannah Reid's vocals are both therial and powerful by turns - I was reminded of Judy Tzuke in some places and Annie Lennox in others - and she radiates emotional tenderness and integrity.  I'm just an old git now, but listening to this, I was suddenly nursing a bruised and yearning young heart again.<br /><br />This is a little bit of class, and I suspect will become one of those albums whose sound defines a year for many people.  Very warmly recommended.

I fell in love with this band from the first five seconds of \\"Tall Poppy Syndrome\\". It was that immediate. They are all the best darkness, melody, and beauty of Opeth meshed with all the best wacky time signatures and jazzy influence of Dream Theater or AaL. For me, at least, that's perfection.<br /><br />I loved \\"Bilateral\\". I missed some of the more straight-forward prog of TPS, but that album was complete. It was almost like a pop album for a metal band but not in a bad way. I mean that in the best possible fashion. Think Katatonia \\"Night is the New Day\\". The vocals were beautiful, the arrangements entrancing, and the tempos combined the best of metallic aggression and jazz-induced complication and experimentation. Songs like \\"Mediocrity Wins\\" and \\"Forced Entry\\" were absolute groove fests. If you're head isn't moving while listening to those, you don't have a pulse. The complete change to a more accessible, yet still nuanced style made me very, very happy.<br /><br />Now for \\"Coal\\". This album, as almost everyone has mentioned, it darker. It's not \\"ZOMG!!! EVIL VOCALS!!!\\" darker. It's more heavy in the atmosphere like classic 90's doom. Don't get me wrong. There is plenty of upbeat stuff to go around and the vocals are pretty much completely clean this time around, but the arrangements are clearly more heavy in tonality and focused. There is much less dynamic riffage per song and things seem more direct than ever. The usage of heavy atmosphere using the keys, deep and heavy distortion, and otherworldly vocal harmonies creates a feeling they have rarely administered to their sound thus far.<br /><br />My favorite song on the album, \\"The Valley,\\" has quite possibly the most haunting vocal melodies ever in a Leprous song, the very reason I love the song so much. It is a nine minute slow build into a crescendo that is undoubtedly as metal as anything they've ever done. Then the final two songs welcome their long-time friend Ihsahn back and show the more aggressive side of the album. Largely though, I'd have to say this album is less aggressive than either of their last two offerings. Large portions of songs(2-4 minutes in segments) are mostly vocals. It's somewhat like Opeth's \\"Damnation\\" in that way, as one reviewer mentioned.<br /><br />The only reason I knock a star of is because I was expecting a bit more. This is merely personal preference but I loved their style on both of their last two albums and I'd just like to see a bit more experimentation. This one is much more direct and, while it has its amazing moments, I just wish it was a tad more like their previous efforts. Either way you cant go wrong. Leprous is on a stage all their own. No one sounds like them and they copy no one. I don't think they'll ever do anything I don't thoroughly enjoy. Please come to Boston ASAP!!!doubtedly as metal as anything they've ever done. 1781162646|4.0|I'm a sucker for Stephen King it seems. No matter the topic, the fright-factor, nor the era: I'm a sucker for Stephen King.<br /><br />His short crime-noir \\"Joyland\\" is a wonderful read. Always so evocative of place, time, and his characters' life-stage, King has built another undeniably real, and relatable world in an early 70's down-stage amusement park.<br /><br />The story zeros in on a rising college senior who's spending his summer working at Joyland, a small Carolina coastal amusement park, that's more akin to traveling town carnival than a Disney theme park.<br /><br />Devin Jones is an aspiring writer (quite a surprise, right?). Reflecting on what was simultaneously the best and worst summer of his life, he comments, \\"When you're twenty-one, life is a roadmap. It's only when you get to be twenty-five or so that you begin to suspect you've been looking at the map upside down, and not until you're forty are you entirety sure.\\"<br /><br />King bakes a supernatural mystery within a softer, larger murder mystery - several years ago, a young woman was killed during a ride on in Joyland's haunted house. The whodunit is interested, but merely drives Jones ride to self-realization and his emotional growth from young man, to adult.<br /><br />The story contains love, and lost love: both precisely described with King's astute insight, and subtle hand. And he still writes a child's perspective that connects with me like no other author.<br /><br />He's known as a `horror' writer, but the truth is, King writes about human nature. The horror, the mystery, the crime, are simply the vehicle to convey what makes us human, and he does so in a way that creates an intimate relationship between author, reader, and character.<br /><br />I thoroughly enjoyed \\"Joyland\\"...the conclusion to the murder-mystery itself ends a little flat, but the rest of the story more than makes up for it.

0307352153|5.0|I don't mean that extroverts can't create the culture and technology that define modern scoiety - but, in disproportionate numbers, the introverts do. So why hasn't society created more niches (as opposed to ghettos) where we can be happy and productive?<br /><br />Cain has done a great job of opening the discussion - and it's a complex one. Introversion is not a uniquely human trait. Just as some human children are knee-huggers and some not, we see the same among our cousins in the great apes and among our more distant relations. And, some of the liveliest and most engaging people you could hope to meet are introverts at heart. Oh, they can go out and interact as closely as anyone, but time spent that way comes at a price, paid in time away from the \\"madding crowd,\\" restoring the thoughts that have been tugged in too many directions by too many people. I found myself and most people I know described clearly in this book's various explorations of introversion. But, although I consider myself as introverted as any, a surprising number of extrovert charactetistics seemed to match, too. It's not a simple or one dimensional phenomenon, which just makes it more interesting.<br /><br />Despite generally well informed and well researched discussion, at least one serious misunderstanding marred Cain's discussion. On p.147, Cain talks about exploratory vs. shyer personalities among guppies, depending on prevalence of predators. Although she notes how little time it takes \\"... for their genes to mutate\\" in adapting to a change of predation, the mutation happened way back. What she describes comes not from new updates to DNA, but from co-existing versions of genes waxing and waning in the population, according to the survival advantage each confers. (Although people rightly bemoan loss of species, loss of gene variations within species carries all the same dangers.)<br /><br />In general, though, Cain offers helpful suggestions about introversion, both for those who live it and those who live with it. She speaks from experience, not just her own but that of others who study introversion or succeed in an extroverted world anyway. She also offers some travel notes from her forays into the most aggressively extroverted of worlds, the Tony Robbins motivational training seminars. Truly a bizarre and alien experience for someone like me to hear about. But then, I've been fortunate in living among thought-workers for my adult life, in academia and hi-tech industry. Those few who don't have a strong native streak of introversion in those careers have learned the basic survival skills in dealing with us.<br /><br />The basic message comes through loud and clear, despite not being loud at all. Introverts, not that any one crisp line defines the term, form a major part of the world. Our role models are easily shouted down, shadowed, or left in the background. But they're there, every vert, intro or extro, needs to understand our needs and our ways of interacting, and once those are accomodated, the very great gifts we bring to those around us. (My wife once said she like nerd guys. Maybe it's hard for a lady to get their attention - but, when she does, they bring an intensity of attention to her that's not equalled anywhere else.)<br /><br />-- wiredweirdd those who live with it. B00AYB0TOW|4.0|Being a long time fan of John Wagner's Judge Dredd comic, I came into this movie with little to no expectations.  Comic book fans have been forced into a sort of prolonged cynicism in regards to movie adaptations.  So many poor movie makers, like Chris Nolan and his ilk have been granted access to these properties with little to no oversight or knowledge until now, comic movies are by and large purely trash.  This movie is an exception.  I took away the feeling that the makers of this movie were actually fans and somewhat knowledgeable of the very things that make comic books enjoyable and vital.<br />Not to say that this is an accurate depiction of an actual comic book.  In all fairness though, I don't see how a movie could be.  It is like the difference between Star Wars and a Moebius comic.  The movies were visually styled after his artwork but really look nothing like it.  So instead the makers gave us the \\"feel\\" of Judge Dredd.  The style in which they worked is very John Carpenter and it is a perfect fit.  Recreating this in a digital format is in itself quite an accomplishment.  Their ability to mimic the look of 1970's-80's Carpenter action movies within a brand new filming technique is a great sign for all filmmakers.  The technology seems to work well.<br />Karl Urban does a fantastic job of portraying Joe Dredd.  He adeptly captured the stoicism of the character with simple lines and excellent physical acting.
